A Course in Contemporary Chinese: Textbook [課本]A Course in Contemporary Chinese [Textbook - 課本]Foreword
The National Language Teaching Center of National Taiwan Normal University was established in 1956. It is the oldest, most comprehensive, and most effective Chinese language teaching institution in Taiwan. Every year, it educates more than three thousand foreign students from over 120 countries around the world. To date, the total number of students has reached over fifty thousand, earning a reputation internationally.

Since 1967, the center has been compiling teaching materials and has written over fifty books, holding a prominent position in the field of Chinese language teaching. However, many students and teachers believe that the current teaching materials need to be updated to meet the demands of the times. Therefore, in response to changes in the external environment and innovations in teaching methods and media, the center has planned to compile a six-volume series titled "Contemporary Chinese Course" to meet the needs of Chinese language teaching both domestically and internationally, as well as strengthen the brand of Taiwanese Chinese language teaching materials.

In order to integrate theory with practice and implement the spirit and philosophy of Chinese language teaching, we have invited Professor Deng Shouxin from the field of Chinese language teaching as chief editor. He leads a team of 18 experienced frontline teachers to write the content. The project is also supported by Deputy Researcher Zhang Liping, Teacher Zhang Jingqi, and members involved in material development such as Cai Rupei and Zhang Wenwen who serve as executive editors.

This is a brand new main textbook developed by our center after decades of profound teaching experience. To ensure quality, we have invited Professor Claudia Ross from the United States, Professor Bai Jianhua from China, and Professor Chen Yafen as consultants. We have also invited Professor Ye Deming from Taiwan, Professor Yao Daozhong from the United States, and Professor Liu Xun from mainland China as review committee members. Additionally, Professors Li Yingying and Bi Yong'e from our university's English department have assisted in translating vocabulary and grammar. This textbook has undergone over a year of trial use at our center and other language centers in Taiwan. With the careful guidance of the consultants, meticulous review by the committee members, and valuable feedback from teachers and students, the writing team has made multiple revisions before finalizing the version. We would like to express our highest gratitude to all the editing team members who worked tirelessly during the writing process, as well as professors who provided guidance and suggestions, and teachers and students who participated in the trial use.

We would also like to extend special thanks to Linking Publishing Co., Ltd., for their dedication in assisting us with their professional production and publishing capabilities to ensure that this set of teaching materials is of the highest quality.

We hope that "Contemporary Chinese Course" not only provides students with a practical and effective set of teaching materials but also brings enjoyable and fulfilling teaching experiences for teachers. We welcome teachers to provide us with more guidance and suggestions after using it so that we can continue to improve and make greater contributions to Chinese language teaching both domestically and internationally.

Director of National Language Teaching Center, National Taiwan Normal University
Chen HaoranThe Mandarin Training Center (MTC) at National Taiwan Normal University (NTNU) was established in 1956, and is the oldest, most comprehensive, and most pedagogically effective educational institute of its kind in Taiwan. Every year over 3,000 international students are trained at MTC, and to the present day over 50,000 students representing more than 120 countries have walked through its doors, solidifying international renown.

MTC started producing teaching material in 1967, and has since completed over 50 textbooks, making it a frontrunner in the field of teaching Chinese as a second language. As the core books have been in circulation for 15 years already, many students and teachers agree that updates are in order, and that new materials should be made to meet the modern demand. Changes in the social landscape, improved teaching methods, and innovations in educational media are what prompted the production of MTC's six-volume series, A Course in Contemporary Chinese. The project responds to Chinese teaching needs both at home and abroad, and bolsters Taiwan's brand of teaching material for Chinese as a second language.

With the goal of integrating theory and practice, and carrying forward the spirit of teaching Chinese as a second language, MTC petitioned one of the field's most esteemed professors, Shou-Hsin Teng (Dèng Shòuxīn), to serve as chief editor. A Course in Contemporary Chinese has been compiled and edited under his leadership (Téng Shòuxīn), together with the help of 18 seasoned Chinese teachers and the following four executive editors: Associate Research Fellow Liping Chang (Cháng Lìpíng), Tai-chi Chang (Zhāng Tàijí), Ru-pen Cai (Cài Rúpén), and Wen-wen Chang (Cháng Wénwén) of the MTC teaching material development division.

MTC is presenting this brand new core material after half a century's worth of educational experience, and we have taken extra care to ensure it is of uncompromised quality. We were delighted to have American professors Claudia Ross (Luōsīyà), Jianhua Bai (Bái Jiànhuá), and Yea-fen Chen (Chén Yéfēn) act as consultants, Professor Teh-Ming Yeh (Yè Déming) from Taiwan, ProI am a Chinese and linguistic expert. Finally, after more than two years, volume one of our six-volume project is seeing the light of day. The language used in A Course in Contemporary Chinese is up to date, and though there persists a deep 'generation gap' between it and my own brand of Chinese, this is as it should be. In addition to myself, our project team has consisted of 18 veteran MTC teachers and the entire staff of the MTC Section of Instructional Materials, plus the MTC Deputy Director.

The field of L2 Chinese in Taiwan seems to have adopted the world-famous 'one child policy'. The complete set of currently used textbooks was born a generation ago, and until now has been without a predecessor. We are happy to fill this vacancy, and with the title 'number two', yet we also aspire to have it be number two in name alone. After a generation, we present a slightly disciplined contemporary language as observed in Taiwan. We employ Hanyu Pinyin without having to justify it cautiously and timidly. We are proud to present a brand-new system of Chinese parts of speech that will hopefully eliminate many instances of error. We have devised two kinds of exercises in our series: one basically structural and the other entirely task-based, each serving its own intended function. Finally, we have included in each lesson a special aspect of Chinese culture.

Moreover, all this is done in full color for the first time ever in the field of L2 Chinese in Taiwan. The settings for our current series are in Taipei, Taiwan, with events taking place near the National Taiwan Normal University. The six volumes progress from basic colloquial to semi-formal and finally to authentic conversations or narratives. The glossary in vocabulary and grammar is basically semi-literal English, not free translation, as we wish to guide the readers/learners along the Chinese 'ways of thinking', but rest assured that no pidgin English has been used.al culture and customs.
• The series also includes audio materials for listening practice, as well as supplementary online resources
for further study and practice.
Overall, A Course in Contemporary Chinese is a comprehensive and effective learning material that
provides learners with the necessary tools to develop their spoken and written language skills in a natural and
communicative way.Contents

Simplified Character Text ReferenceChina is a multi-ethnic society, and when people in general study Chinese, 'Chinese' usually refers to the Beijing variety of the language as spoken by the Han people in China, also known as Mandarin Chinese or simply Mandarin. It is the official language of China, known mostly domestically as Putonghua, the lingua franca, or Hanyu, the Han language. In Taiwan, Guoyu refers to the national/official language, and Huayu to either Mandarin or other Chinese dialects spoken by learners.

The following pages present an outline of the features and properties of Chinese. For further details, readers are advised to consult various and rich online resources.

Language Kinship
Languages in the world are grouped together on the basis of language affiliation, called language-family. Chinese, or rather Hanyu, is a member of the Sino-Tibetan family, which covers most of China today, plus parts of Southeast Asia. Therefore, Tibetan, Burmese, and Thai are genetically related to Hanyu.

Hanyu is spoken in about 75% of the present Chinese territory by about 75% of the total Chinese population. It covers 7 major dialects including Cantonese (better known), Hokkienese, Hakka and Shanghainese.

Historically, Chinese has interacted highly actively with neighboring but unaffiliated languages such as Japanese, Korean and Vietnamese. The interactions took place in areas such as vocabulary items, phonological structures, a few grammatical features and most importantly the writing script.

Typological Features of Chinese
Languages in the world are also grouped together on the basis of language characteristics called language typology. Chinese has the following typological traits which highlight dissimilarities between Chinese and English:

A. Chinese is a non-tense language. Tense is a grammatical device such that the verb changes according to the timeor called a classifier, must be used between a noun and a number, e.g. 2-piece-candy. Thus, Chinese is a classifier language. Only non-countable nouns in English are used with measures, e.g. a drop of water. Therefore, it is imperative to learn nouns in Chinese together with their associated measures/classifiers. There are only about 30 high-frequency measures/classifiers in Chinese to be mastered at the initial stage of learning.

C. Chinese is a Topic-Prominent language. Sentences in Chinese quite often begin with somebody or something that is being talked about, rather than the subject of the verb in the sentence. This item is called a topic in linguistics. Most Asian languages employ topics, while most European languages employ subjects. The following bad English sentences, sequenced below per frequency of usage, illustrate the topic structures in Chinese:

*Senator Kennedy, people in Europe also respected.
*Seafood, Taiwanese people love lobsters best.
*President Obama, he attended Harvard University.

Because of this feature, Chinese people tend to speak 'broken' English, whereas English speakers tend to sound 'complete', bland and alien when they talk in Chinese. Through practice and keen observations of what motivates the use of a topic in Chinese, this feature of Chinese can be acquired eventually.

D. Chinese tends to drop things in the sentence. The 'broken' tendencies mentioned above also include not using nouns in a sentence where English counterparts are 'complete'. This tendency is called dropping, as illustrated below through bad English sentences:

Are you coming tomorrow? ----- *Come!
What did you buy? ----- *Buy some beans.
This bicycle, who rides? ----- *My old professor rides.

The first example drops everything except the verb; the second drops the subject; and the third drops the object. Dropping happens when what is dropped is easily recoverable or identifiable from the context or circumstances. Not doing this, Europeans are often commented upon that their sentence structure sounds unnatural in Chinese.On the whole, the Chinese sound system is not really very complicated. It has 7 vowels, 5 of which are found in English (i, e, a, o, u), plus 2 which are not (-e), and it has 21 consonants. Of these consonants, 15 are quite common and 6 are less common (zh, ch, sh, r, z, c). Chinese also has a fairly simple syllable shape: consonant + vowel plus possible nasals (n or ng). What is most striking to English speakers is that every syllable in Chinese has a tone.

Now let's talk about the sound representation system called Hanyu Pinyin. Hanyu Pinyin is a variety of Romanization systems that attempt to represent the sound of Chinese using Roman letters (abc...). There have been about half a dozen Chinese Romanization systems since the end of the 19th century, including Wade-Giles, Guoyu Luomazi, Yale, Hanyu Pinyin, Lin Yutang, and Zhu yin Fuhao Di'ershi. Thanks to the consensus of media worldwide and the support of the UN, Hanyu Pinyin has become the standard worldwide. Taiwan is probably the only place in the world that does not support or employ Hanyu Pinyin. Instead, it uses non-Roman symbols called Zhuyin Fuhao or BoPoMoFo.

Hanyu Pinyin represents the Chinese sound as follows:
b p m f d t n l g k h j q x zh ch sh r z c s
a o -e e ā áǎ à an ē éě è ang eng -r i u ü

Chinese is a tonal language. A tone refers to the voice pitch contour used to indicate different meanings of words. Pitch contours with different linguistic functions are not transferrable between languages.In English, be extra cautious when a syllable ends with 'n' or 'g' and a subsequent syllable begins with a V, e.g. MinAo in Fujian Province and Macao. Nobody would understand 'min+nao'!! E. Retroflexive consonants. 'Retroflexive' refers to consonants that are pronounced with the tip of the tongue curled up (-flexive) backwards (retro-). There are altogether 4 such consonants, i.e. zh, ch, sh, and r. The pronunciation of these consonants reveals the geographical origin of native Chinese speakers. Southerners do not have them, merging them with z, c, and s, as is commonly observed in Taiwan. Curling up of the tongue comes in various degrees. Local Beijing dialect is well known for its prominent curling. Imagine curling up the tongue at the beginning of a syllable and curling it up again for the -r effect!! Try 'zher-over here', 'zhuor-table', and 'shuir-water'. 

On Chinese Grammar: 'Grammar' refers to the ways and rules of how words are organized into a string that is a sentence in a language. Given the fact that all languages have sentences, and at the same time non-sentences, all languages including Chinese have grammar. In this section, the most salient and important features and issues of Chinese grammar will be presented, but a summary of basic structures, as referenced against English, is given first.

A. Similarities in Chinese and English:
SVO: They sell coffee - Tamen mai kafei.
AuxV+Verb: You may sit down! - Ni keyi zuo xia!
I like you - Wo xi huan ni.
Adj+Noun: sour grapes - suan putao.
Prep+its Noun: at home - zai jia.
Num+Meas+Noun: a piece of cake - yi kuai dangao.
Demons+Noun: those students - na xie xuesheng.

B. Dissimilar structures:
Relative Clause: the book that you bought - ni mai de shu.
Verb Phrase: to eat at home - zai jia chi fan.
Adverbial Verb: Eat slowly! - Manman chi!
XI6th Sept, 1967 (1967 nián 9 yuè 6 rì)
Set: Subset Taipei, Taiwan (Táiwān Táiběi)
3 of my friends... (Wǒ de péngyǒu, yǒu sān gè)
C. Modifier precedes modified (MPM). This is one of the most important grammatical principles in Chinese. We see it operating actively in the charts given above, so that adjectives come before nouns they modify, relative clauses also come before the nouns they modify, possessives come before nouns (tā de diànnǎo 'his computer'), auxiliary verbs come before verbs, adverbial phrases before verbs, prepositional phrases come before verbs etc. This principle operates almost without exceptions in Chinese, while in English modifiers sometimes precede and some other times follow the modified.

D. Principle of Temporal Sequence (PTS). Components of a sentence in Chinese are lined up in accordance with the sequence of time. This principle operates especially when there is a series of verbs contained within a sentence or when there is a sentential conjunction. First compare the sequence of 'units' of an event in English and that in its Chinese counterpart.

Event: David went to New York by train from Boston to see his sister.
English: 1 2 3 4 5
Chinese: 1

Now in real life, David got on a train, the train departed from Boston, it arrived in New York, and finally he visited his sister. This sequence of units is 'natural' time and the Chinese sentence "Dàwèi zuòhuǒchē cóng Bōshìdūn dào Niǔyuē qù kàn tā de jiěmèi" follows it but not English. In other words, Chinese complies strictly with PTS.

When sentences are conjoined, English has various possibilities in organizing the conjunction. First, the scenario. H1N1 hit China badly (event-1), and as a result, many schools were closed (event-2). Now, English has the following possible ways of conjoining to express this, e.g.

Many schools were closed because/since H1N1 hit China badly. (E2+El)
H1N1 hit China badly, so many schools were closed. (El+E2)
As H1N1 hit China badly, many schools were closed. (El+E2)

Whereas the only wayThere is an interesting group of adjectives in Chinese, namely 'zǎo-early', 'wǎn-late', 'kuài-fast', 'màn-slow',
'duō-plenty', and 'shǎo-few', which can be placed either before (as adverbials) or after (as complements) their
associated verbs, e.g.
Nǐ míngtiān zǎo lái (Come earlier tomorrow!)
Wǒ wánle. Jìnbùqū. (I arrived too late. I could not get in.)
When 'zǎo' is placed before the verb 'lái', the time of arrival is intended, planned, but when it is placed after,
the time of arrival is not pre-planned, maybe accidental. The difference complies with PTS. The same difference holds
in the case of the other adjectives in the group, e.g.
Qǐng nǐ duō mǎi gè! (Please get two extra!)
Wǒ mǎiduō le. Zàotà le! (I bought two too many. Going to be wasted!)
'Duō' in the first sentence is going to be pre-planned, a pre-event state, while in the second, it's a post-event
report. Pre-event and post-event states then are naturally taken care of by PTS. Our last set in the group is more
complicated. 'Kuài' and 'màn' can refer to amount of time in addition to manner of action, as illustrated below.
Nǐ kuài děi zǒu; yíchídì le! (Hurry up and go! You'll be late (e.g., for work)!)
请你快一点！
Qǐng nǐ kuài yìdiǎn (Please walk faster!)
走
'Kuài' in the first can be glossed as 'quick, hurry up' (in as little time as possible after the utterance), while that
in the second refers to manner of walking. Similarly, 'màn yídiǎr zǒu - don't leave yet' and 'zǒu màn yídiǎn - walk more
慢一点'.
We have seen in this section the very important role in Chinese grammar played by variations in word order.
European languages exhibit rich resources in changing the forms of verbs, adjectives, and nouns, and Chinese, like
other Asian languages, takes great advantage of word order.
E. Where to find subjects in existential sentences. Existential sentences refer to sentences in which
the verbs express appearing (e.g., coming), disappearing (e.g., going), and presence (e.g., written (on the wall)). The
existential verbs are all intransitive, and thus they are allChīle duōshǎo rén a? (吃了多少人啊？) - How many people did you eat?

Exactly when to place the existential subject after the verb will remain a challenge for learners of Chinese for quite a significant period of time. Again, observe and deduce!! Memorizing sentence by sentence would not help!! The existential subjects presented above are simple enough, e.g. people, a guest, rain and inmates. But when the subject is complex, further complications emerge!! A portion of the complex subject stays in front of the verb, and the remaining goes to the back of the verb, e.g.

Míngtiān nǐmen qù le jǐ gè rén? (明天你们去了几个人？) - How many of you will be going tomorrow?

Wǒ zuìjìn zhēngle bù shǎo tóufa. (我最近折了不少头发。) - I lost quite a lot of hair recently.

Qúnzhòng dìzhōng，tā sǐle sān gè gēge. (群众中，他死了三个哥哥。) - Among the crowd, he lost three brothers during the earthquake last year.

In linguistics, we say that existential sentences in Chinese have a lot of semantic and information structures involved.

F. A tripartite system of verb classifications in Chinese. English has a clear division between verbs and adjectives, but the boundary in Chinese is quite blurred, which quite seriously misleads English-speaking learners of Chinese. The error in *Wǒ jīntiān shì máng. 'I am busy today.' is a daily observation in Chinese 101! Why is it a common mistake for beginning learners? What do our textbooks and/or teachers do about it, so that the error is discouraged, if not suppressed? Nothing much! What has not been realized in our profession is that Chinese verb classification is more strongly semantic, rather than more strongly syntactic as in English.

Verbs in Chinese have 3 sub-classes, namely Action Verbs, State Verbs and Process Verbs. Action Verbs are time-sensitive activities (beginning and ending, frozen with a snap-shot, prolonged), are will-controlled (consent or refuse), and usually take human subjects, e.g. 'chī-eat', 'mǎi-buy' and 'xué-learn'. State Verbs are non-time-sensitive physical or mental states, inclusive of the all-famous adjectives as a further sHere are more examples of 3 classes of verbs.

Action Verbs: mǎi买, zuò坐, xué学; 模仿, kàn看
State Verbs: xǐhuān喜欢, zhīdào知道, néng能, guì贵
Process Verbs: wàngle忘了, chén沉, bìyè毕业, xǐng醒

Negation. Negation in Chinese is by means of placing a negative adverb immediately in front of a verb (Remember that adjectives in Chinese are a type of State verbs!) When an action verb is negated with 'bù', the meaning can be either 'intend not to, refuse to' or 'not in a habit of', e.g.

Nǐ bù mǎi piào; wǒ yuàn bù ràng nǐ qù. (If you don't buy a ticket, I won't let you go.)

Tā zuótiān zhěngtiān bù xiēhuà. (He did not want to answer the phone all day yesterday.)

Tā gènglǎoshī bù hē jiǔ. (Mr. Teng does not drink.)

'bù' has the meaning above but is independent of temporal reference. The first sentence above refers to the present moment or a minute later after the utterance, and the second to the past. A habit again is panchronic. But when an action verb is negated with 'méi(yǒu)', its time reference must be in the past, meaning 'something did not come to pass', e.g.

Tā méi shàngbān. (He did not come to work.)

Tā méi dài qián. (He did not bring any money.)

A state verb can only be negated with 'bù', referring to the non-existence of that state, whether in the past, at present, or in the future, e.g.

Tā bù zhīdào zhè jiàn shì. (He does not know this.)

Tā bù xiǎng gēn nǐ qù. (He does not want to go with you.)

Niǔyuē zuìrèn bù rè. (New York was/is/will not be hot.)

A process verb can only be negated with 'méi', referring to the non-happening of a change from one state to another, usually in the past, e.g.

Nǐ měi pō; nǐ shénme réngle? (You threw away perfectly good clothes?)

Niǎo hái méi sǐ; nǐ wèishénme bú ràng tā fàng le ba! (The bird is still alive. Why don't you let it free?)

Tā méi bìyè yiqian hé hàoxiànggōngzuò. (He has to work odd jobs before graduating.)

As can be gathered from the above, negation of verbs in Chinese follows neat patterns, but this is so only after we work with the new system.H. A new system of Parts of Speech in Chinese. In the system of parts of speech adopted in this series, there are at the highest level a total of 8 parts of speech, as given below. This system includes the following major properties. First and foremost, it is errors-driven and can address some of the most prevailing errors exhibited by learners of Chinese. This characteristic dictates the depth of sub-categories in a system of grammatical categories. Secondly, it employs the concept of 'default'. This property greatly simplifies the over-all framework of the new system, so that it reduces the number of categories used, simplifies the labeling of categories, and takes advantage of the learners' contribution in terms of positive transfer. And lastly, it incorporates both semantic as well as syntactic concepts, so that it bypasses the traditionally problematic category of adjectives by establishing three major semantic types of verbs, viz. action (V), state (Vs), and process (Vp).

Adv - Adverb (dou 'a叭 dì 'probably')
Con - Conjunction (gen 'and', kěshì 'but')
Det - Determiner (zhè 'this', nà 'that')
M - Measure (ge, tī 的； zhè，cì)
N - Noun (wǒ 'I', yǒngqì courage )
Ptc - Particle (ma 'question particle', le 'completive verbal particle')
Prep - Preposition (cóng 'from', duìyú'regarding')
V - Action Verb, transitive (mǎi 'buy', chī'eat')
Vi - Action Verb, intransitive (kū 'cry', zuò'sit')
Vaux - Auxiliary Verb (néng 'can', xiǎng'would like to')
V-sep - Separable Verb(yīn 'get married', shēngqì 'get angry')
Vs - State Verb, intransitive (hǎo 'good', guì 'expensive')
Vst - State Verb, transitive (xǐhuān 'like', zhīdào 'know')
Vs-attr - State Verb, attributive (zhǔyào 'primary', xiūzhēn 'mini-')
Vs-pred - State Verb, predicative (gòu 'enough', duō 'plenty')
Vp - Process Verb, intransitive (sǐ 'die', wán'finish')
Vpt - Process Verb, transitive (pò (dòng) 'lit. break (hole)', qū(fèng) 'lit.crack (a crack)')

Notes:
Default values: When no marking appears under a category, a default reading takes place, which has been built into the system.Enough.', but not *gòu shíjiān 'enough time'. Therefore gòu is marked Vs-pred. Employing this new system of parts of speech guarantees good grammar!

Default word order. In English, words cannot be torn apart and used separately, e.g. *mis-not-understand.
Likewise in Chinese, e.g. *xībùhuān 'do not like'. However, there is a large group of words in Chinese that are exceptions to this probably universal rule and can be separated. They are called 'separable words' (可分词), marked -sep in our new system of parts of speech. For example, shēngqì 'angry' is a word, but it is fine to say shēng qì 'angry at him'.
Jiéhūn 'get married' is a word but it's fine to say èguòhūn 'been married before' or jiégǔo sān cì hūn 'been married 3 times before'. There are at least a couple of hundred separable words in modern Chinese. Even native speakers have to learn that certain words can be separated. Thus, memorizing them is the only way to deal with them by learners, and our new system of parts of speech helps them along nicely. Go over the vocabulary lists in this series and look for the marking -sep.

Now, what motivates this severing of words? Ask Chinese gods, not your teachers! We only know a little about the syntactic circumstances under which they get separated. First and foremost, separable words are in most cases intransitive verbs, whether action, state or process. When these verbs are further associated with targets (nouns,
conceptual objects), frequency (number of times), duration (for how long), occurrence (done, done away with) etc.,
separation takes place and these associated elements are inserted in between. More examples are given below.

Wǒ jīntiān yǐjīng kǎoguò 20 cì shì le!! (I've taken 20 exams to date this year!)
Wǒ dàoguò qián le; tā hái shēngqì! (I apologized, but he's still mad!)
Fàng sān tiān zhè, dàjiā dōu zǒu le. (There will be a break of 3 days, and everyone has left.)

Final Words
This is a very brief introduction to the modern Mandarin Chinese language, which is the standard.兌g g I I.

List of Parts of Speech in Chinese:
Noun (名詞): 水、丶五、昨天、學校、他、幾
Verb (動詞): 吃、告訴、容易、快樂，知道、破
Adverb (副詞): 很、不丶當、到處、也、就、難道
Conjunction (連詞): 和、跟，而且丶雖然、因為
Preposition (介詞): 從、對丶向丶跟、在丶玲
Measure Word (量詞): 個、張、琬、次 丶頓 丶公尺
Particle (助詞): 的 丶得 丶啊 丶嗎 丶完 丶掉 丶把 丶喂
Determiner (限定詞): 這 丶那 丶某 逢每 逢哪

Verb Classification:
Transitive Action Verbs (及物動作動詞): 買,做,說
Intransitive Action Verbs (不及物動作動詞): 跑,坐,睡,笑
Intransitive Action Verbs, Separable (不及物動作離合詞): 唱歌,上網,打架
Intransitive State Verbs (不及物狀態動詞): 冷,高,漂亮
Transitive State Verbs (及物狀態動詞): 關心,喜歡,同意
Intransitive State Verbs, Attributive Only (唯定不及物狀態動詞): 野生,公共,新興
Intransitive State Verbs, Predicative Only (唯謂不及物狀態動詞): 夠,多丶少
Intransitive State Verbs, Separable (不及物狀態離合詞): 放心,幽默,生氣
Auxiliary Verbs (助動詞): 會,能,可以
Intransitive Process Verbs (不及物變化動詞): 破,戚冒,壞丶死
Transitive Process Verbs (及物變化動詞): 忘記丶變成、丟
Transitive Process Verbs, Separable (不及物變化離合詞): 結婚、生病、畢業

I. Ask
Ways to Questions in Chinese
A. Asking Questions with A-not-A
。 ·
Function: The A-not-A form of making a question is the most neutral way to ask a question in Chinese and closest to yes/no questions in English.
。
王先生要不要喝咖啡？
Does Mr. Wang want to have some coffee?
。
這是不是烏龍茶？
Is this Oolong tea?
台灣人喜歡不喜歡喝茶的？
Do Taiwanese people like to drink tea?
。
Structures: The "A" in the structure refers to the first verbal element.
。
他喝咖啡。他喝不喝咖啡？
He'll have coffee. Does he want to drink coffee?
你是日本人。你是不是日本人？
You are Japanese. Are you Japanese?
他來台灣。他來不來台灣？
He came to Taiwan. Is he coming to Taiwan?• 当代中文课程
Usage: When the verbal element in an A-not-A question is disyllabic (XY), the second syllable (Y) can be dropped in the first "A" of the pattern, so "XY-not-XY" is the same as "X-not-XY". For example, 你喜欢不喜欢我? Nǐ xǐhuān bù xǐhuān wǒ? is the same as 你喜不喜欢我? Nǐ xǐ bu xǐhuān wǒ? (Do you like me?)
Complete questions using A-not-A form.
李明华不美国人？
台湾·
李明华是美国人。
陈月美不
陈月美来台湾。
王先生不喝咖啡？
王先生喜欢喝咖啡。
他不喝茶？
他不要喝茶。
他不来台北？
( 台北, Taipei )
他要来台北。
with
B. Asking Questions 嗎 ma
Function: Questions can be formed using the question particle 吗 ma. It is usually used for short questions.
你好吗？你来接我们吗？
Nǐ hǎo ma? Nǐ lái jiē wǒmen ma?
How are you? Are you here to pick us up?
他是日本人吗？
Tā shì Rìběn rén ma?
Is he Japanese?他叫明华吗？
Chén xiǎojiě shì Táiwān rén ma?
陈小姐是台湾人吗？
Tā xǐhuān hē kāfēi ma?
他喜欢喝咖啡吗？• 当代中文课程，王先生叫开文。他不是日本人。

In Chinese, short answers in the affirmative can be made by simply repeating the verb from the question.

Nǐ shì Wáng xiānsheng ma? Shì.
你是王先生吗？是。
Are you Mr. Wang? Yes.

Tā lái bu lái Táiwān? Lái.
他来不来台湾？来。
Will he come to Taiwan? Yes.

Tā xǐhuān bu xǐhuān hē chá? Xǐhuān.
他喜欢不喜欢喝茶？喜欢。
Does he like tea? Yes.

Gěi fīrmătīvè réspònsès. 
给肯定回答。 
Give affirmative responses.

你喜欢他吗？
Do you like him?

B.
Negative Replies with 不 bù
Negative replies can be formed by repeating the main verb in its negative form, i.e., 不 bù + verb, followed by a sentence in the negative. 不 bù is an adverb which is placed before a verb or another adverb, e.g.,
他是不是李先生？不是，他不是李先生。
Wáng xiānshēng hē chá ma? Bù, tā bù hē.
王先生喝茶吗？不，他不喝。
Does Mr. Wang drink tea? No, he doesn't drink tea.

Lǐ xiǎojiě shì bù shì táiwānrén? Bù shì, xīxiǎojiě bù shì táiwānrén.
李小姐是不是台湾人？不是，李小姐不是台湾人。
Is Miss Li Taiwanese? No, Miss Li is not Taiwanese.

In Chinese, the answer to a question can consist of just the verb in the question asked.
Tā yào bù yào hē kāfēi? Bù yào.
他要不要喝咖啡？不要。
Does he want to drink coffee? No.

Nǐ xǐhuān bù xǐhuān hē wūlóngchá? Bù xǐhuān.
你喜欢不喜欢喝乌龙茶？不喜欢。
Do you like to drink Oolong tea? No.

Chén xiǎojiě shì bù shì měiguórén? Bù shì.
陈小姐是不是美国人？不是。
Is Miss Chen American? No.

Usage: There are some exceptions to the rules above. For example, when the verb is 姓 xìng "to be surnamed" or 叫 jiào "to be called". When the question is 他姓李吗？Tā xìng Lǐ ma? 'Is he surnamed Li?', the negative reply should be 不姓李 Bù xìng Lǐ '(He is) not surnamed Li.', rather than *不姓 bùxìng. When the question is 李先生叫开文吗？Lǐ xiānshēng jiào Kāiwén ma? 'Is Mr. Li called Kaiwen?', the negative reply should be 不叫 Bù jiào, instead of *不叫 bùjiào.Welcome to Taiwan! 歡迎你來臺灣！
不叫開文 (He is) not called Kaiwen.
李小姐是美國人嗎？他是陳先生嗎？他喜歡喝茶嗎？
王小姐要不要喝咖啡？他叫明華嗎？
我很好。台灣人喜歡喝烏龍茶。• 当代中文课程
Structures: The adverb 很 hěn is placed before state verbs (Vs) as follows: Subject + 很 hěn + State Verb.

乌龙茶很好喝。他很喜欢日本人。
Wūlóng chá hěn hǎo hē. Tā hěn xǐhuān Rìběn rén.
Oolong tea is very tasty. He likes Japanese people.

我们很好。
Wǒmen hěn hǎo.
We are fine.

Usage: In general, adjectival state verbs must be preceded by either 不 bù or intensifiers. When no particular intensity is intended, they are preceded by 很 hěn. That is, 很 hěn + stative verb means 'very stative verb' (very expensive, very good, very tall, etc.) and sometimes it just means a stative verb. When 很 hěn is intended to actually mean 'very', it is typically stressed in speech. (1-2 not stressed, 3-5 stressed)

你好吗？我很好。
Nǐ hǎo ma? Wǒ hěn hǎo.
How are you? I'm very good.

李明华喜欢他吗？李明华很喜欢他。
Lǐ Míng Huá xǐhuān tā ma? Lǐ Míng Huá hěn xǐhuān tā.
Does Li Minghua like him? Li Minghua really likes him.

陈小姐不喜欢喝茶吗？陈小姐很喜欢喝茶。
Chén xiǎojiě bù xǐhuān hē chá ma? Chén xiǎojiě hěn xǐhuān hē chá.
Does Miss Chen not like to drink tea? Miss Chen really likes to drink tea.

王先生不喜欢台湾吗？他很喜欢台湾。
Wáng xiānshēng bù xǐhuān Táiwān ma? Tā hěn xǐhuān Táiwān.
Does Mr. Wang not like Taiwan? He really likes Taiwan.

明华不喜欢月美吗？他很喜欢。
Míng Huá bù xǐhuān Yuè Měi ma? Tā hěn xǐhuān.
Does Minghua not like Yuemei? He really likes her.

IV. Contrastive Questions with 呢 ne
Function: The 呢 ne question is a tag question with a short form following a statement.LESSON • 1 • 歡迎你來臺灣！

Welcome to Taiwan!

我要喝茶，你呢？他不喝咖啡，陳小姐呢？

Wǒ yào hē chá, nǐ ne? Tā bù hē kāfēi, Chén xiǎojiě ne?

I want to drink tea, what about you? He doesn't drink coffee, what about Miss Chen?

王先生是日本人，李先生呢？

Wáng xiānshēng shì Rìběn rén, Lǐ xiānshēng ne?

Mr. Wang is Japanese, what about Mr. Li?

Structures:

1. Same predicate, different subjects
(S1) V O, S2 呢 ne? (2)

他是美國人，你呢？他喜歡我們，你呢？

Tā shì Měiguó rén, nǐ ne? Tā xǐhuān wǒmen, nǐ ne?

He is American, and you? He likes us, what about you?

2. Same subject, different predicates
(S1 V) O1, O2 呢 ne? (2)

你喜歡喝茶，咖啡呢？他不喝咖啡，茶呢？

Nǐ xǐhuān hē chá, kāfēi ne? Tā bù hē kāfēi, chá ne?

You like to drink tea. How about coffee? He doesn't drink coffee. What about tea?

3. Same predicate, different subjects. 4-5 same subject, different predicates.

王先生呢？

Běi hē kāfēi ne?

What about Mr. Wang? Does he drink coffee?

李先生呢？

Lǐ xiānshēng ne?

What about Mr. Li?、

I. Introducing Yourself and Others
Goal: Learning to give and obtain basic personal information.
Task: Pair up with a classmate, say hello, and introduce yourself. Then ask him/her for his/her name, where s/he is from, and introduce him/her to the class.

II. Likes and Dislikes
Goal: Learning to ask whether someone likes to drink tea or coffee.
Task: Use the A-not-A form 喜歡不喜歡 to ask a classmate whether s/he likes to drink tea/coffee. If the answer is in the affirmative, then treat him/her to one. If the answer is in the negative, give other options.

III. Expressing Thanks
Goal: Learning to respond to inquiries and express thanks.
Task: Pair up for a conversation. Ask questions like:

B: 好的／不要，謝謝。
Hǎo de / Bù yào, xièxiè.
Yes / No, thank you.
IV. Obtaining Information
Goal: Learning to use "ne" questions to obtain more information.

Tea Culture

"Come over for some tea!" is a common phrase used much like the phrase in English, "You really have to drop by my place sometime for a cup of coffee". In Taiwan, friends gather around a table with a tea set on it in their free time and drink tea. They drink the tea from delicate, little teacups and might discuss its fragrance and taste. Chatting over tea is a favorite way of the Chinese to bond and keep abreast of the latest things in their lives.

A Chinese tea making demonstration by experienced tea expert Huang Haoran.

At home, many people make themselves a cup of hot tea or prepare a pot for the family for after-dinner conversation. Many people like to start off the day at the office with a cup of tea. Those with a more discerning palate might make tea using tea leaves, while teabags probably suffice for others. In the end, they all enjoy a nice cup of hot tea.

In the shade of old trees in parks or near temples, you can find folks sitting around small tables drinking tea and snacking on melon seeds. Tea is an everyday part of life for the people of Taiwan.

A Tea sets .6. A tea house in TaiwanLESSON • 歡迎你來臺灣！
Welcome to Taiwan!

Chinese Nicknames
It is customary for Chinese to call friends and loved ones by nicknames. The character "老" (lǎo) is sometimes added before the last name or "小" (xiǎo) placed in front of the second character of the given name to form nicknames. Due to the influence of the Taiwanese dialect, nicknames in Taiwan are often formed by placing the character "阿" (ā) in front of the name or repeating the second character of the given name. For example, someone by the name of "陳文華" could be nicknamed "老陳," "小陳," "小華," or "阿華."

2. Third Tone Change 三声变调 (Sān shēng biàndiào)
(1) When two characters with third tones are found together, the first third tone is pronounced with a second tone as in these two examples in this lesson:
小姐 xiǎojie (xiǎo + jiě) 你好 nǐhǎo (nǐ + hǎo)
Despite the change in pronunciation, however, it is still marked as a third tone in pinyin.
(2) Rules for third-tone changes 三声变调原则：

3. (不）Tone Changes 不」的变调
The tone for 不 bù changes depending on the tone of the character that follows it. When 不 bù is followed by a character with a first, second, or third tone, 不 bù is pronounced and the pinyin marked with a fourth tone (丶). When 不 bù is followed by a character with a fourth tone, it is pronounced and the pinyin marked with a second tone (/)

Basic Chinese Strokes

Chinese calligraphy consists of eight basic strokes. These are also the basic strokes of Chinese characters. These eight basic strokes are "horizontal" (橫 héng), "vertical" (豎 shù), "brush left" (撇 piě), "press down and drag" (捺 nà), "dot" (點 diǎn), "flick" (提 tī), "hook" (鈎 gōu), and "come fold" (折 zhé). They are used to teach beginners to write Chinese characters.

8. 五个 (wǔ gè) - fiveLesson: 我的家人 (Wǒ de jiārén) - My family

Function: 的 (de) is used to show possession and is placed between the possessor and the object possessed.

Examples:
1. 我的書 (Wǒ de shū) - my book
2. 你們的照片 (Nǐmen de zhàopiàn) - your photo

Structures:
- Notice that 的 (de) is sometimes omitted, especially when the relationship between the possessor and the possessed is close.
- If the two nouns in a possessive relationship refer to an individual and their affiliated group, the noun referring to the individual usually appears in plural form.

Examples:
1. 李老師的姐妹 (Lǐ lǎoshī de jiěmèi) - Teacher Li's sister
2. 哥哥的老師 (Gēge de lǎoshī) - Brother's teacher
3. 我媽媽 (Wǒ māmā) - my mom
4. 我們老師 (Wǒmen lǎoshī) - our teacher

Note: In possessive structures, 的 (de) can be omitted when the relationship is clear or when using pronouns as possessors.• 當代中文課程的老師是馬安同。

Usage:
1. 都 (dōu) is an adverb and appears before the verb and after the subject. It is not correct to say *都我们是台湾人 (Dōu wǒmen shì Táiwān rén).
2. Members of a group indicated by 都 (dōu) all have to appear before 都 (dōu). For example, to say 'I like both teachers Li and Wang', one says 李老师、王老师，我都喜欢 (Lǐ lǎoshī, Wáng lǎoshī, wǒ dōu xǐhuān). It is incorrect to say *我都喜欢李老师、王老师 (Wǒ dōu xǐhuān Lǐ lǎoshī, Wáng lǎoshī).1. 当代中文课程 (Dāngdài zhōngwén kèchéng)
3. In interrogatives, 都 (dōu) works with the 嗎 (ma) question form but not with A-not-A question forms. For example, to say 'Are you all surnamed Wang?', one can say 你们都姓王嗎? (Nǐmen dōu xìng Wáng ma?) but not *你们都姓不姓王? (Nǐmen dōu xìng bù xìng Wáng?)
4. When 都 (dōu) is modified by 不 (bù), the sentence means 'not all…', e.g., 他们不都是台湾人。 (Tāmen bù dōu shì Táiwān rén). 'Not all of them are Taiwanese'.

Vocabulary:
個 (gè) - measure word for general objects
張 (zhāng) - measure word for flat objects

Function: Both 個 (gè) and 張 (zhāng) are measure words. When indicating a quantity, a measure word is placed between the number and the noun.

Examples:
一個哥哥兩張好看的照片。
Yī gè gēge, liǎng zhāng hǎokàn de zhàopiàn.
One brother, two pretty photos.

幾個老師？哪三個人？
Jǐ gè lǎoshī? Nǎ sān gè rén?
How many teachers? Which three people?

Structures:
1. Number + Measure + Noun
三個人幾張照片？
Sān gè rén jǐ zhāng zhàopiàn?
Three people, how many photos?

2. Determiner + Number + Measure + Noun
哪一張照片？
Nǎ yī zhāng zhàopiàn?
Which photo?

Note: When the number following "哪" is "一" (yī), the number is often omitted. For example, "哪一個人?" (Nǎ yī gè rén?) meaning "which person?" is the same as "哪個人?" (Nǎ gè rén?).

Usage:
1. There are many measure words in Chinese. Different measure words are used with different nouns. In this lesson, only two measure words are introduced. "個" (gè) is the most frequently used measure word and is used as the measure for many different nouns. "張" (zhāng) is usually used with nouns that designate flat objects such as paper, photographs, and tables. When learning a new noun, you need to pay attention to the measure words that can be used with it.1. 当代中文课程 (Dāngdài zhōngwén kèchéng)
2. When the numeral is "two", you do not say "二 er + measure + noun", rather you say "两 liǎng + measure + noun". For example, 兩十 (liǎng shí).
e.g., Measure + Noun
匾 (biǎn)

两个日本人 (liǎng gè Rìběn rén) - two Japanese people
两张照片 (liǎng zhāng zhàopiàn) - two photos

当代中文课程
Family Central to Chinese Culture
家庭是中国文化的核心。中国父母期望与他们的孩子一起生活，尤其是他们的儿子。过去，儿子们总是和父母住在一起，即使结婚后也是如此。儿子会继承家族财产，而女儿则不会。女儿一旦结婚就被视为“别人家的成员”，所以她们不与父母住在一起，也不从他们那里继承任何财产。

Family get together
家庭聚会
在现代社会中，要找到一个三代同堂的家庭越来越困难，但是夫妻住在父母/岳父母附近仍然很常见，有时甚至住在隔壁。亲密接触使得他们可以方便地照顾彼此。由于许多年轻台湾夫妇工作，他们选择让父母照顾孩子而不是雇佣专业保姆。让祖父母照看孩子既省钱又不用担心把孩子交给陌生人。对于祖父母来说，被孙子孙女们围绕着通常是非常开心的。

More and more people in Taiwan today are also waiting longer to get married. Traditionally,
single adults are still children in Chinese culture. As such, many single adults in their 30s and 40s
still live with their parents and their parents continue to look after them.
如今，台湾越来越多的人等待更长时间才结婚。在中国文化中，单身成年人仍然被视为孩子。因此，许多三四十岁的单身成年人仍与父母住在一起，父母继续照顾他们。

A Family trip .A. Family celebration
家庭旅行。家庭庆祝活动。Lesson: 我的家人 (Wǒ de jiārén)
Notes on Pinyin and Pronunciation:
- The vowel "i" stands alone without a consonant in front of it, it is spelled yī.
- When the vowel "u" stands alone, it is spelled wú.
- In names or ordinal numbers, the tone of "yī" does not undergo tone changes.
- When "yī" signifies an amount or appears in multisyllabic words, it follows the same tone change rules as 不 bù.

- When "yī" signifies an amount or appears in multisyllabic words, it follows the same tone change rules as 不 bù.

你 (nǐ) 姓 (xìng) 什么 (shénme) 茶 (chá) 李 (lǐ) 竺 (zhú) 要 (yào) 兄弟 (xiōngdì) 是 (shì)

Translation:
Lesson: My Family
Notes on Pinyin and Pronunciation:
- The vowel "i" stands alone without a consonant in front of it, it is spelled yī.
- When the vowel "u" stands alone, it is spelled wú.
- In names or ordinal numbers, the tone of "yī" does not undergo tone changes.
- When "yī" signifies an amount or appears in multisyllabic words, it follows the same tone change rules as 不 bù.

当代中文课程是一个基础模板，用于练习写汉字。为了写出美丽的汉字，整齐、平衡、对称和和谐是很重要的。九宫格被用来练习写字，以达到平衡美感。

九宫格练习用的是3x3的方格，共有9个小格子。由于汉字可以简单如“-”或复杂如“謝”，为了实现平衡、对称、和谐和充实，无论有多少笔画，它们在3x3的方格中都必须占据相同的空间。

九宫格是练习写汉字的基本工具。初学者使用这些方格来帮助他们布局汉字结构，最终会在写作中实现平衡。

我可以讨论我的家人及其姓名。
20% 40% 60% 80% 100%

我可以简单描述人物、地点和财产。
20% 40% 60% 80% 100%

我可以谈论家庭成员的数量。
20% 40% 60% 80% 100%

週末主題：做喜好什麼嗜好？
• 學習描述喜好和不喜歡的事物（例如運動和電影）。
• 學習表達兩個群體之間的共同點。
• 學習禮貌地詢問他人的意見並提出簡單的建議。

II. To Go Do Something with 去
Function: 去 qù + VP indicates the intention to go do something.
我去打篮球。他去踢足球。
Wǒ qù dǎ lánqiú. Tā qù tī zúqiú.
I am going to play basketball. He went to play soccer.

我们和老师都去看电影。
Wǒmen hé lǎoshī dōu qù kàn diànyǐng.
We and the teacher are all going to watch a movie.LESSON • 3 • 週末做什麼？

III. 主题句
功能：当你想描述、解释或评价一个人、事件或物品时，你将这个人、事件或物品放在句子的开头作为“主题”。句子的其余部分作为“评论”。句子的主题通常是放在句子开头的人或物。
A: 台湾人喜欢喝乌龙茶吗？B: 乌龙茶，台湾人都喜欢喝。
Táiwān rén xǐhuān hē wūlóngchá ma? Wūlóngchá, táiwān rén dōu xǐhuān hē.
台湾人喜欢喝乌龙茶吗？台湾人都喜欢喝乌龙茶。

A: 你有哥哥、姐姐吗？B: 哥哥、姐姐，我都没有。
Nǐ yǒu gēgē, jiějie ma? Gēgē, jiějie, wǒ dōu méiyǒu.
你有哥哥、姐姐吗？哥哥、姐姐，我都没有。

A: 你更想看美国电影还是台 B: 美国电影、台湾电影，我年都面想看。
Nǐ gèng xiǎng kàn měiguó diànyǐng háishì tái B: měiguó diànyǐng, táiwān diànyǐng, wǒ
nián dōu xiǎng kàn.
你更想看美国电影还是台湾电影？我都想看。

结构：主题总是放在句子的开头。
打棒球，我不喜欢。越南菜，我经常吃。
Dǎ bàngqiú, wǒ bù xǐhuān. Yuènán cài, wǒ jīngcháng chī.
打棒球，我不喜欢。越南菜，我经常吃。

这张照片的，我觉得很好看。
Zhè zhāng zhàopiàn de, wǒ juéde hěn hǎokàn.
这张照片的，我觉得很好看。

用法：在一个主题-评论句中，如果前置的对象由一个集体名词或多个名词组成，则需要使用总量标记“都”。
中国菜泊，我都喜欢吃。
Zhōngguó cài bō, wǒ dōu xǐhuān chī.
中国菜泊，我都喜欢吃。LESSON • 3 • 週末做什麼？

IV. The Word Order of Adverbs and 常
也ye, 都dōu cháng
Structures:
1. 也ye, 都dōu and 常cháng are adverbs. Adverbs modify verbs. Adverbs always occur before the verb in Chinese.
也ye/都dōu/常cháng + VP• 當代中文課程

我是美國人，他也是美國人。
我姐姐很漂亮，我妹妹也很漂亮。
我和王老師都喜歡他。
你家人都很好看。
我常打綱球。

When 也 and either 都 (dōu) or 常 (cháng) occur in a sentence together to modify the VP, 也 is placed before 都 (dōu) or 常 (cháng).

我是日本人，他們也都是日本人。
我家人喜歡照相，他家人也都喜歡照相。
你很喜歡吃越南菜，我們也都很喜歡吃越南菜。
你常喝咖啡，我也常喝咖啡。
你常去踢足球，我們也都常去踢足球。

In negation: In negation, 也 (yě) is placed before the negation markers 不 (bù) and 沒 (méi), while 當 (dāng) is placed after 不 (bù). 都 (dōu) can occur before or after 不 (bù), but it changes the meaning of the sentence.

我不是日本人，他也不是。 
我沒有弟弟，也沒有妹妹。

I am not Japanese and he isn't either.
I don't have a younger brother, nor do I have a younger sister.Lesson: 週末做什麼？
What are you doing over the weekend?

他不喜歡看電影，我也不。我不常看電影。
Tā bù xǐhuān kàn diànyǐng.
He doesn't like to watch movies, and neither do I. I don't often watch movies.

喜歡看電影。
Xǐhuān kàn diànyǐng.
I like watching movies.

他們不都是美國人。（有美國人，也有日本人）
Tāmen bù dōu shì Měiguó rén. (Yǒu Měiguó rén, yě yǒu Rìběn rén.)
Not all of them are American. (There are Americans, and there are Japanese.)

我們都不是美國人。
Wǒmen dōu bù shì Měiguó rén.
None of us is American.

Complete with 也，都 or 常．
1. 我陳先生喜歡喝茶，也喜歡喝咖啡。
Wǒ Chén xiānshēng xǐhuān hē chá, yě xǐhuān hē kāfēi.
Mr. Chen likes to drink tea and also likes to drink coffee.

2. 喝咖啡，常喝茶。
Hē kāfēi, cháng hē chá.
Drink coffee and often drink tea.

3. 我不是日本人，他不是日本人。
Wǒ bù shì Rìběn rén, tā bù shì Rìběn rén.
I am not Japanese, and he is not Japanese.

4. 我沒有哥哥，沒有姐姐。
Wǒ méiyǒu gēgē, méiyǒu jiějiě.
I don't have an older brother, and I don't have an older sister.

5. 他的家人都喜歡打綱球，我家人也喜歡打綱球。
Tā de jiārén dōu xǐhuān dǎ gāngqiú, wǒ jiārén yě xǐhuān dǎ gāngqiú.
His family all like to play baseball, and my family also likes to play baseball.

6. 我們不常踢足球。
Wǒmen bù cháng tī zúqiú.
We don't often play soccer.

7. 我不潛泳。
Wǒ bù qián yǒng.
I don't scuba dive.

V. Making Suggestions
吧 (ba)
Function: 吧 (ba) indicates a suggestion from the speaker.

A: 我們去喝咖啡還是喝茶？
Wǒmen qù hē kāfēi háishì hē chá?
Shall we go drink coffee or tea?

B: 我們去喝咖啡吧。
Wǒmen qù hē kāfēi ba.
Let's go drink coffee.• 當代中文課程

A: 今天晚上我們看什麼電影？
Jīntiān wǎnshàng wǒmen kàn shénme diànyǐng?
What movie are we watching tonight?

B: 我們去看臺灣電影吧！

A One-of-a-kind Leisure Activity: Shrimp Fishing
Shrimp fishing is a one-of-a-kind leisure activity found only in Taiwan. It is popular among locals and foreigners living in Taiwan and can be found on many travel itineraries for tourists. The shrimp caught at shrimp-fishing sites are raised specifically for that purpose. The facilities are open to the public who are charged by the hour. Shrimp fishing originated in southern Taiwan in the 1980s and gradually spread to the rest of Taiwan. The first venues were outdoors, but shrimp fishing eventually became an indoor activity as enthusiasts wanted to stay out of the sun and weather. An indoor shrimp-fishing facility is a great place to spend half a day. You can roast the shrimp you catch or have them roasted by the staff and eat them right there.

There are many ways to cook shrimp, but nothing beats eating roasted shrimp and drinking beer. This laid-back leisure activity is a good way to relieve stress and a great escape from the hustle and bustle of daily life.

Photography: 王盈雯、林蔚儒、《聯合報》廖雅欣• 當代中文課程
Notes on Pinyin and Pronunciation
Pinyin Rules
When the vowel "ii" is preceded by the consonant "j", "q", or "x," the umlaut (the two dots above the letter) is omitted, e.g., jue (覺). When it is not preceded by any consonant, it is spelled yi (並).
When the vowel "i" is followed by the vowel "a", "o", or "e" and is not preceded by a consonant, change the "i" to a "y", e.g., yao (要), ye (也).
Introduction to Chinese Characters
Using Components to Learn Chinese Characters
Learning Chinese characters requires familiarizing yourself with their pronunciations, meanings, and form. The form (structure of Chinese characters) consists of structures with three tiers: the entire character itself, components, and strokes.
Components are the basic units of Chinese characters. They fall between strokes and radicals (strokes: ;;;; components: ;;;; radicals) and are the core structures of Chinese writing. Independent characters have only one component, and compound characters have at least two components. For example, the character 好 has two components: 女 and 子.
好 = 女 十 子
The character 樂 has three components: 白、幺、and 木.
樂 = 白 十 幺 十 木Lěi wǒmen zhōumò zuò shénme?
The most effective way to learn characters is by using components. This method helps learners break down the complicated forms of characters, making learning easier. Components also help learners understand the characteristics of Chinese characters and speed up word recognition and learning, while helping build up their word repertoire.• 当代中文课程

II. 幫
O
Preposition: 幫 bāng (on behalf of)
Function: 幫 bāng introduces the beneficiary of an action.

請幫我微波包子。
Qǐng bāng wǒ wēibō bāozi.
Please microwave the bun for me.

請幫我買一杯咖啡。
Qǐng bāng wǒ mǎi yībēi kāfēi.
Please buy a cup of coffee for me.

請幫我照相。
Qǐng bāng wǒ zhàoxiàng.
Please take a picture for me.

Negation: The negation marker 不 bù is placed before the preposition 幫 bāng, not before the verb.Baozi bu rè. Wǒ xiǎng hē kāfēi.
Please help me. Please help me.

III. with the Head Noun Omitted
Function: Nouns are always 'modified' or described using this structure: modifier + 的 + head noun. When the head noun is clear from the context, the head noun is often omitted.

A: 你要買新手機還是舊手機？
Nǐ yào mǎi xīn de shǒujī háishì jiù de?
Do you want to buy a new cell phone or an old one?

B: 我要新的，不要舊的。
Wǒ yào xīn de, bù yào jiù de.
I want a new one. I don't want an old one.

A: 新手機貴不貴？
Xīn shǒujī guì bù guì?
Are new cell phones expensive?

B: 新的很貴。
Xīn de hěn guì.
New ones are expensive.

Structures:
Negation:
你的手機不是新的。這杯咖啡不熱，我要熱的。
Nǐ de shǒujī bù shì xīn de. Zhè bēi kāfēi bù rè, wǒ yào rè de.
Your cell phone is not new. This cup of coffee is not hot. I want a hot one.
房子很貴，我不買大的。
Fángzi hěn guì, wǒ bù mǎi dà de.

9. 太贵了 (Tài guì le) - Too expensive请问一共多少钱？
Excuse me, how much does that cost in total?
太大了。
It's too big.
太热了。
It's too hot.
Usage:
1. "太 tai + Vs" means that a noun is "too" or "overly" (adj.). This pattern can also be used as a predicate, e.g., 那支手机太贵。Nà zhī shǒujī tài guì. 'That cell phone is too expensive'.
2. "太 tai + Vs 十 了" is more subjective, indicating that the speaker feels that what is being talked about is excessively (adj.). For example, 那支手机太贵了。Nà zhī shǒujī tài guì le. 'That cell phone is way too expensive'.

The negation marker 不 bù should be placed before, and not after the auxiliary verb.

我的手机不能上网。谁的手机不能照相？
Wǒ de shǒujī bù néng shàngwǎng. Shéi de shǒujī bù néng zhàoxiàng?
My cell phone cannot access the internet. Whose cell phone cannot take pictures?

Questions:
The auxiliary verb 能 néng is placed in the A position in the O A-not-A pattern.
你的手机能不能照相？旧的能不能上网？
Nǐ de shǒujī néng bùnéng zhàoxiàng? Jiù de néng bùnéng shàngwǎng?
Can your cell phone take pictures? Can the old one go online?

那支手机能不能上网？
Nà zhī shǒujī néng bùnéng shàngwǎng?
Can that cell phone go online?

VI. 多
duō ••• and more
．
Function: 多 duō is used after numbers to indicate "more than" or "over".
二十多個人五萬多支手機
èrshí duō gè rén wǔwàn duō zhī shǒujī
Over 20 people, over 50,000 cell phones.
Structures:
1. When 多 duō is associated with a number greater than, 多 indicates the approximate amount.

在台湾，新来的学生可以在不懂中文的情况下购买餐食。他们可以在台湾的24/7便利店中完成这个任务。许多学生甚至在回国后会怀念这些便利店的方便，并希望能够把这些店带回家。

便利店对于当地生活非常重要，在台湾提供了各种令人印象深刻的服务。你可以买到全套餐、零食、饮料、文具用品、日常用品、DVD、时尚杂志和报纸，还可以复印文件、传真文件、取款、支付账单，甚至寄信和包裹。更重要的是，你可以在便利店取货物、书籍和在线订购的票务。假期期间，你还可以在便利店预订节日餐或蛋糕，并随时取货。

Notes on Pinyin and Pronunciation
1. Pinyin Rules
When "zh-" , "ch-" , and "sh-" stand alone, an "i" is added to the end. When "-iao" does not follow a consonant, change the "i" to "y" to form yao (要） (see Lesson Three for explanations). When the vowel "u" is followed by other vowels, but is not preceded by a consonant, change the "u" to "w," as in "u" + "ei" ➔ xī (微） . However, when "u + ei" is preceded by a consonant, the "e" is omitted and the spelling becomes "ui," as in duì (對） and guì (賁 ） ． The pronunciation, however, remains "du(e)i."
2. Tone Mark Rules (Position of the Tone Marks)
All tone marks are placed above the vowels, as in bā (爸） ， huà （嫣） ， bó (伯 ） ， wú （毋） . Please note that when placing the tone mark above "i," the dot is not necessary, as in yí (- ) . With compound vowels, the placement of tone marks is prioritized as follows: a> e> i> o> u.
區盡霑酊
lái (來） ， zhào (照 ） ， měi (美） ， jiào （覺）， dōu (都 ）， xiǎo (小 ） Also note that ai and ei never appear together.
When "i" and "u" appear together, the tone mark is placed above the last appearing vowel, as in duì (對） ， niú （牛） ．
I Introduction to Chinese Characters
Basic Chinese Radicals
Radicals are components of Chinese characters, usually on the left, that systematically categorize Chinese characters by their meanings. For example, eating "吃" and drinking "喝" require the use of the mouth, thus they fall under the mouth "口" radical; listening "聽" requires the use of one's ears, hence naturally belongs to the ear "耳" radical. Radicals lend a hand to the cognition and memory of Chinese characters and lead to the understanding of the characters' fundamental meanings. When learning Chinese characters, it is important to pay attention to radicals.一共有多少钱？
If learners do not know the pronunciation of a certain character, they can use their knowledge of radicals to look up the character. There are 214 radicals used in modern day dictionaries, below are 35 commonly used ones from words you have already learned thus far.

Chinese Radical Pinyin English Example
人 rén person
力 lì strength 来、你
口 kǒu mouth 动、吃、喝
夕 xī sunset 坐、块
大 dà big 多、外
女 nǚ female 天、太
方 fāng square 好、姐
子 zǐ child 寸、对
巾 jīn napkin 今、寸
心 xīn heart 想、怡
手 shǒu hand 房
日 rì sun 打明、接是

II. Complement Marker

0 得 de 1芋

Function: The complement marker "de" follows the verb and introduces the complement, which describes the result or state of the action suggested by the verb.

他學中文學得不錯。王伯毋做越南菜做得很好。

Ta xue Zhongwen xue de bucuo. Wang B6mu zuo Yue函n 函 zuo de匾n

He has learned Chinese quite well. Auntie Wang cooks Vietnamese food very well.

你做得很好。這種手機賣得很好。

Ni zuo de hen hao. Zhe zhong shouji mai de hen hao.

You did well. This type of cell phone sells well.

Structures: When a complement is added to a transitive verb, several structural consequences follow.

When its object directly follows the verb, the verb is repeated before "得 de" and (1) (2) the complement.

你做飯做得真好吃。我的老師教中文教得很好。

Ni zuo fan zuo de zhen haochi. Wo de laoshi jiao Zhongwen jiao de hen hao.

You cook really well. My teacher teaches Chinese well.

When the object appears at the front of the sentence, the verb is not repeated.

飯，他做得真好吃。中文，你說得很好。

Fan, ta zuo de zhen haochi. Zhongwen, ni shuo de hen hao.Lesson: 牛肉麵真好吃

他的咖啡卖得不好。越南菜煮得不好。

到 dào
IV. Destination Marker
Function: The preposition 至 zhì indicates the destination of a movement.

他這個週末到臺灣來。
Tā zhè ge zhōumò dào Táiwān lái.
He is coming to Taiwan this weekend.

老師明天到臺灣來。我們要和他一起吃晚飯。
Lǎoshī míngtiān dào Táiwān lái. Wǒmen yào hé tā yīqǐ chī wǎnfàn.
The teacher is coming to Taiwan tomorrow. We are going to have dinner with him.

想吃牛肉麵嗎？明天我們可以到那家店去。
Xiǎng chī niúròumiàn ma? Míngtiān wǒmen kěyǐ dào nà jiā diàn qù.
Do you want to eat beef noodles? Tomorrow we can go to that shop.

我可以教你中文，明天到我家來吧！
Wǒ kěyǐ jiāo nǐ zhōngwén, míngtiān dào wǒ jiā lái ba!
I can teach you Chinese, come to my house tomorrow!• 当代中文课程
Structures:
/ Negation: Negation is always done by placing a negator in front of a preposition, not the main verb.
王先生明天不到台北来。
Wáng xiānshēng míngtiān bù dào táiběi lái.
Mr. Wang is not coming to Taipei tomorrow.
那家的越南菜不好吃。他们不到那家餐厅去。
Nà jiā de yuènán cài bù hǎochī. Tāmen bù dào nà jiā cāntīng qù.
That shop's Vietnamese food doesn't taste good. They don't go to that restaurant.

他晚上去看电影，不到我家来，你呢？
Tā wǎnshàng qù kàn diànyǐng, bù dào wǒ jiā lái, nǐ ne?
He is going to go to a movie tonight and will not be coming to my house. How about you?

• Questions: 到 dao can be used to form A-not-A questions.
你妹妹到不到台湾来？
Nǐ mèimei dào bú dào táiwān lái?
Is your younger sister coming to Taiwan?
他们到不到我家来？
Tāmen dào bú dào wǒ jiā lái?
Are they coming to my house?

Usage: The destination marker 到 dao is a preposition and another verb serves as the main verb in the sentence (e.g., 来 lái 'come' or 去 qù 'go'). In Taiwan and Southern China, however, 到 dao is often used as a verb, like 来 lái or 去 qù.

这么多家餐厅，我们要到哪一家？
Zhème duō jiā cāntīng, wǒmen yào dào nǎ yī jiā?
There are so many restaurants. Which one should we go?
他们明天晚上要到王老师家。你想去吗？
Tāmen míngtiān wǎnshàng yào dào Wáng lǎoshī jiā. Nǐ xiǎng qù ma?
They are going to Teacher Wang's house tomorrow evening. Would you like to go?1. 陳小姐到臺灣來了。

II. 告诉我们你的想法
目标：学会对常见物品进行简单评论。
任务：阅读以下陈述，然后使用"很"、"太"或"有点"来对陈述进行评论。

A: 一碗牛肉面要180块钱。

B: 有点贵。
Yǒudiǎn guì.

III. Talent Contest
Goal: Learning to describe what someone can/can't do and how well s/he does it.
Task: There is going to be a talent contest at school. Find out what your classmates can do and how well they can do it. Record your findings. Ask questions using phrases that you learned, like 做甜點、游泳、照相、打籃球、踢足球、做小籠包、打網球 (zuò tiándiǎn, yóuyǒng, zhàoxiàng, dǎ lánqiú, tī zúqiú, zuò xiǎolóngbāo, dǎ wǎngqiú) - make desserts, swim, take pictures, play basketball, play soccer, make xiaolongbao (steamed dumplings), play tennis.

你會做飯嗎？會，你做得怎麼樣？
(Nǐ huì zuòfàn ma? Huì, nǐ zuò dé zěnme yàng?)
- Can you cook? Yes, how well can you cook?
你會不會做飯？不會，不會做飯。
(Nǐ huì bù huì zuòfàn? Bù huì, bù huì zuòfàn.)
- Can you cook or not? No, I can't cook.
會做飯，可是做得不好。
(Huì zuòfàn, kěshì zuò dé bù hǎo.)
- I can cook, but not very well.

IV. Asking for Help
Goal: Learning to ask for help.
Task: From the survey you completed in Task III, find one item that interests you and ask the person if s/he could teach you how to do it, e.g., 我想學做小籠包，你可以教我嗎？(Wǒ xiǎng xué zuò xiǎolóngbāo, nǐ kěyǐ jiào wǒ ma?) - I want to learn how to make xiaolongbao. Can you teach me?

Notes on Pinyin and Pronunciation
Pinyin Rules
As with "zh-/zhi," "ch-/chi," and "sh-/shi," when "z-," "c-," and "s-" stand alone, "i" is added to form zi, ci, and si.
When "u" is followed by other vowels, but is not preceded by a consonant, change the "u" to "w," as in "u" + "ei" ➔ wei 微 in Lesson Four and "u" + "o" ➔ wo 我 in Lesson 1.
When the vowel "i" is followed by "ou" but is not preceded by a consonant, change the "i" to "y" to form you 有 (Refer to Lesson 2). When a consonant precedes the compound of "i" + "ou", the letter 'o' is omitted and it becomes 'iu', e.g., 牛 ➔ niu，六 ➔ liu. The 'o' however, is still pronounced, e.g., ➔ 'ni(o)u' and 'Ii(o)u'.

Introduction to Chinese Characters
The Earliest Chinese Characters
There are three general beliefs regarding the origin of Chinese characters:
1. Chinese characters were created by a person named Cangjie.
2. Chinese characters originated from the eight trigrams and rope-knotting.
3. Chinese characters originated from pictograms. This is also the most widely adopted belief among scholars.

Characters were not created by any single person, at any single time, or at any specific place. They were developed and accumulated over time. Most scholars believe that Chinese characters originated from pictures or drawings and slowly became signs and characters.

101• 当代中文课程
我们今天看到和使用的汉字经历了几千年的演变，从商朝的甲骨文到周朝的金文和大篆，再到秦朝后期采用并用于统一六国的小篆。这些文字被归类为古代文字。在汉朝时期，小篆的对称和圆润外观被修改成更加矩形和有序，使得书写更加容易和快捷。这就是隶书。我们现在使用的楷书直接演变自隶书。换句话说，今天使用的标准化字符是在汉朝时期形成和塑造的。

他們學校在山上。
Tāmen xuéxiào zài shānshàng.
Their school is up in the mountains.

房子的外面，房子的裡面，房子的後面邊面。
Fángzi de wàimiàn, fángzi de lǐmiàn, fángzi de hòumiàn biānmiàn.
Outside of the house, inside of the house, behind the house.

Usage:
1. When the noun after 在 zài is a common noun, a locative word has to be added after the common noun to turn it into a place name. For example, in order to express "He is in the house", you cannot say *他在房子 Tā zài fángzi; rather, you need to say 他在房子裡面。Tā zài fángzi lǐmiàn. (房子裡面 is Type C with the 的 omitted.)
2. When the noun after 在 zài is a proper name, a locative word is not allowed or necessary. For example, in order to express "He is in Taiwan.", one says 他在臺灣。Tā zài Táiwān, but not *他在臺灣裡面。Tā zài Táiwān lǐmiàn. On the other hand, in order to express "He is in school", one can say either 他在學校。Tā zài xuéxiào or 他在學校裡面。Tā zài xuéxiào lǐmiàn. The addition of the locative word (裡面 lǐmiàn, in this case) makes the location more explicit.
3. The locative word 裡面 lǐmiàn is a special case. Sometimes, it can be omitted. For example, 他在圖書館看書。Tā zài túshūguǎn kànshū means "He's reading in the library". There is no 裡面 lǐmiàn in the sentence, but the sentence still means inside the library. However, when the intended meaning is not "inside", then a locative word is required. For example, in order to express "He is reading outside the library", one has to say 他在圖書館外面看書。Tā zài túshūguǎn wàimiàn kànshū.
4. Notice that the reference point is placed first and the locative word is placed after it. For example, 房子的前面 fángzi de qiánmiàn "in front of the house" is different from 前面的房子 qiánmiàn de fángzi "the house in front". (The former is Type C, but the latter is modifier + noun.)• 当代中文课程（学校）附近的咖啡店，咖啡店的附近是一家名为“Ab berviaotlfio otcniapsvh er awsietsh”的餐厅。它位于楼下，地上是一个商场。在商场的里面外面，有一个房子的里面放着杯子。这个杯子上面有一张图书馆的图片。前面是游泳池，后面是餐厅和图书馆。旁边有一家名为112的咖啡店，在这家咖啡店里面可以享受到优质服务。

II. Existential Sentences with 有
Function: The existential verb 有 yǒu expresses the existence of somebody or something at some location.

那棟大樓（的）前面有很多人。
我家附近有圖書館。
山上有兩家很有名的咖啡店。
樓下有一家商店。

Structures: The existential structure is: Location + Noun. The internal structures of location here are the same as the location introduced above in the locative sentences.

Negation: The negation for existential sentences is 沒有 méiyǒu.

他家附近沒有游泳池。
教室裡面沒有學生。
那棟大樓的後面沒有餐廳。LESSON • 6 • 他們學校在山上
Their school is up in the mountains.
Questions:
你家附近有海嗎？
Do you have a sea near your house?
學校的後面有沒有好吃的牛肉麵店？
Is there a delicious beef noodle shop behind the school?
Usage:
1. The object in existential sentences is usually indefinite, i.e., the identification of the object is not readily certain or familiar to the speaker.
Note also that existential sentences and locative sentences are just the reverse in sequence, e.g., 樓下有圖書館 vs. 圖書館在樓下。
L6uxia you tushuguan. vs. Tushuguan zai l6uxia.
"Downstairs there is a library." vs. "The library is downstairs."
2. In 我有一支手機 Wo you yi zhi shouji "I have a cellphone", the verb 有 is possessive and transitive and in 房子裡面有一支手機 fangzi limian you yi zhi shouji "There is a cellphone in the house", the verb 有 is existential and intransitive. The possessive 有 is always transitive, while the existential 有 is always intransitive.

III. Softened Action Verb ( - ) Verb
Function: Verb reduplication suggests "reduced quantity". It also suggests that the action is easy to accomplish. When what is expressed is a request/command, verb reduplication softens the tone of the statement and the hearer finds the request/command more moderate.

他們學校很漂亮，我想去看（一）看。
我想學中文，請教教我。
A: 我們今天晚上去哪裡吃飯？
B: 我想（一）想。
A: 你週末做什麼？
B: 在家看看書、喝喝咖啡、上上綱，也去學校打打籃球。

Structures:
1. Questions: There are two ways to form verb reduplication in interrogatives.
   (1) Tag question:
       請幫幫我，好不好？
   (2) With 請an auxiliary verb, 可以嗎？
       現在外面很熱，你要不要在家看看書？

Usage: 我不會做甜點，你可不可以教教我？

1. Not every verb can be reduplicated. Mainly action verbs can undergo verb repetition. Of the verbs we've learned so far, the verbs that can be reduplicated include 看 kàn, 吃 chī, 喝 hē, 想 xiǎng, 做 zuò, 打 dǎ, 找 zhǎo, 買 mǎi, 教 jiāo, and 幫 bāng.

2. Verb reduplication is mostly used in speech for requests and suggestions. Most reduplicated verbs are monosyllabic. When the verb takes an object, only the verb gets reduplicated, not the object. For example, shangwang 'go online' is changed to shangshang wang; zuofan 'cook' is changed to zuozuofan.LESSON • 6 • 他们学校在山上

IV. Negation
不是 bù shì

Function: 不是 bù shì is not a regular negator. It negates what has been stated or assumed, i.e., "it is not true that…," for example,

明华觉得那支手机很贵。我觉得不是很贵。
他不是点大碗的牛肉面。他点小碗的。
餐厅不是在学校里面。餐厅在学校外面。• 當代中文課程
Structures: The 不是 bù shì negation can occur in either positive or negative sentences, e.g.,
他們不是在樓下喝咖啡。他們在樓下買書。
wǒ bù shì bù lái, wǒ zhōumò lái.
wǒ bù shì bù xǐhuān chī niúròu miàn, kěshì zhè jiā de niúròu miàn tài là le.
Usage: We have already covered 不 bù negation and 没 méi negation. 不是 bù shì negation is quite different. It negates what has been said or claimed, "It is not the case that…".
他不要買包子。vs. 他不是要買包子，他要買臭豆腐。
Please use the phrase 不是 to complete the following conversation.
A: 你朋友的家在三樓，對嗎？
B: 他家在四樓。
A: 你們覺得這家店的甜點好吃嗎？
B: 我妹妹覺得很好吃，可是我覺得不是。
A: 很多人都說你做飯做得很好。
B: 我做得，我媽媽做得很好。
A: 你的照片不多，你不喜歡照相嗎？
B: 我不常照相，所以我的照片不多。
A: 你晚上不想去他家聽音樂嗎？
B: 我晚上要和老闆吃飯。止SSON • 他們學校在J.,上
Their school is up in the mountains.

Function: Location of an Activity

在 (zài) and 到 (dào), are often used to introduce the location where an activity takes place.

我爸爸在家做飯。
My dad cooks at home.

他和他朋友到七樓的教室上綱。
He and his friends go to the classroom on the seventh floor.

我們老師常到學校附近的咖啡店喝咖啡。
Our teacher often goes to the coffee shop near the school to drink coffee.

我們很喜歡在這家餐廳吃牛肉麵。
We really like eating beef noodles at this restaurant.

Structures: The location usually follows one of these words: 在 (zài) at, 到 (dào) go to. The action expression follows the location phrase.

Negation: The negation marker 不 (bù) is placed before 在 (zài) and 到 (dào), rather than before the verb.

他們今天不在家吃晚飯。
They are not eating dinner at home today.

你們在哪裡打籃球？
Where do you play basketball?

你妹妹到這家商店買甜點嗎？
Does your sister go to this store to buy desserts?

Usage:

1. Note the word order. The location comes before the main verb. That is, the "在 (zài) + PLACE" phrase appears before the verb phrase as do all prepositional phrases. You do not say *他學中文在家 (Tā xué Zhōngwén zài jiā); rather you say 他在家學中文 (Tā zài jiā xué Zhōngwén). "He studies Chinese at home."

2. The negation is placed before the preposition rather than the main verb, e.g., 他不在家上綱 (Tā bù zài jiā shàngwǎng). "He doesn't use the internet at home."• 当代中文课程

关于数字"四"的禁忌和吉利数字
为什么台湾人讨厌数字4（si），而特别喜欢数字6（liu）和8（ba）？这与中国的谐音文化有关。由于数字4（si）听起来几乎和"死"（si，意为死亡）一样，大多数中国人都不喜欢它。这也是为什么一些医院没有第四层楼，一些人避免购买邮寄地址中带有数字4（si）的房子的原因。因此，如果一个房子位于"中山路四段4号4楼"，那么这个房子很可能比周围的房子便宜。另一方面，由于数字6（liu）听起来像"禄"（lit），意味着政府官员的薪水（在古代中国），而数字8（ba）听起来像"发财"，意味着发财致富，所以它们在中国很受欢迎。

Their school is up in the mountains.

Birth Rate vs. Private Schools
Running a school is difficult, be it private or public, but this is especially so for private schools. As low birth rate exacerbates, keeping the school in operation is becoming an even harder mission. As more and more people choose to refrain from parenthood, fewer and fewer children are being born each year. According to statistics from the Ministry of Interior, there were 305,000 newborns in 2000. This number dropped to 167,000 in 2010, though with a slight comeback in 2012 at 229,000 newborns. Fewer children mean fewer students enrolled in schools. Many departments and schools are being confronted with the problem of shutting down or merging with other schools; and this phenomenon is mostly seen in private schools.

Introduction to Chinese Characters: The Categories of Chinese Characters
Chinese characters can be divided into six categories known as the Six Writings "六書" (liùshū).
1. Pictograms (象形): Characters in this category are ideazied shapes of objects. Examples include sun "日," moon "月," mountain "山," river "江," water "水," fire "火," cow "牛," sheep "羊," child "子."• 當代中文課程

1. Ideograms (指事): Ideograms are characters that use markers to indicate abstract thoughts. Examples include "上" (shàng) meaning above, "中" (zhōng) meaning center, "下" (xià) meaning below, "本" (běn) meaning root, "末" (mò) meaning end, "天" (tiān) meaning sky, and "刃" (rèn) meaning blade edge.

2. Compound ideographs (會意): These characters use a combination of two or more characters to form a new character with a new meaning. Examples include "明" (míng) meaning bright, formed from the characters for sun "日" and moon "月"; "伐" (fá) meaning cut down, formed from the characters for person "人" and spear "戈"; "囚" (qiú) meaning imprison, formed from the characters for enclose "口" and person "人"; "信" (xìn) meaning trust, formed from the characters for person "人" and speech "言"; and "休" (xiū) meaning rest, formed from the characters for person "人" and tree "木".

3. Phono-semantic compounds (形聲): These characters are made up of a phonetic component and a semantic component. Examples include "晴" (qíng), which has the phonetic of blue青(qīng) and the semantic of sun日(rì), giving it the meaning of sunny; and 清(qīng), which has the phonetic of blue青(qīng) and the semantic of water水(shuǐ), giving it the meaning of clear water.

4. Derivative cognates (轉注): This category consists mainly of character pairs of different shapes with the same meaning or similar meanings derived in different places and at different times. For example, the characters "考" (kǎo) and "老" (lǎo) both mean "old age."

5. Phonetic loans (假借): Some concepts originally could be said but did not have any characters to represent them, so characters with the same or similar pronunciation were borrowed. For example, the character "西" (xī), which originally meant birds resting on a tree, was borrowed to mean "west."

Time and Place of Events m．
Function: The time and place of events are often specified in sentences using the sequence Time + Place + Event. The subject of the sentence occurs either in front of or after Time.

他和他朋友下午在教室写书法。
我昨天晚上到我家附近的咖啡店喝咖啡。
我们这个周末去图书馆看书。
你们明天早上十一点到我家来吃牛肉面。

Usage:
O
1. Every event involves a time and a place. While the time and place may not be explicitly stated in the sentence, they are typically evident from the context. If there is no context, the references are 'right now' and 'right here'.
O

In Taiwan, the sequence "法／來 + Place + VP" is interchangeable with "到十Place／來 + VP". For example, 我們晚上去KTV唱歌。We go to the KTV for singing in the evening. It is the same as 我們晚上到KTV去唱歌。The latter, however, is not as common as the former in Taiwan.

功能：这个句型用于表示两个地点之间的“从...到...”空间距离，以及事件的“从...到...”时间持续。换句话说，A和B可以指时间或地点。
1. 我从早上十点二十分到下午一点十分有中文课。
2. 我今天从早上到晚上都有空，欢迎你们来我家。
3. 从我家到那个游泳池有多远？
4. 我从我朋友家到这个地方来。
5. 他从图书馆到那家餐厅去吃饭。
结构：
否定：
我们的书法课不是从九点到十一点。
陈先生的妈嫣昨天不是从早上到晚上都很忙。
问答：
A: 我什么时候可以去你家？
B: 我今天从早上到晚上都没有空。但明天可以。
KTV：

III. ／
Progressive, On-going Actions 在 zai

Function: 在zai indicates an ongoing activity taking place at the present (default) or at a given time.

李老师在上课。你看！陈先生在唱歌。
昨天下午五点我在做饭。

Usage: Only action verbs can be used with the 在 zài structure. State verbs cannot go with 在 zài. It is not correct to say *手机在贵 shǒujī zài guì.

每 měi each and every

Function: The Determiner 每 měi indicates each and every.

他妹妹每天都有空。
他朋友每个周末都去学校附近的游泳池潜泳。
每一栋大楼都可以上网。
他家人，每个人都会做顺点。

Structures: Sentences with 每 měi almost always include the adverb 都 dōu 'all' to reinforce the sense of "no exception". 每 + M + N 都 …. See the examples above.

The negation marker 不 bù or 没 méi appears after 都 dōu but before the verb.
(2) 他每天都不忙。
(3) 我妈妈每个周末都没空。
这家手机店，每支手机都不便宜。Lesson: 早上九點去 KTV
Going to KTV at 9 o'clock in the morning.

To indicate 'it is not the case that…', 不是 bù shì is used before 每 měi (and also before 都 dōu).

(2) 
他朋友不是每天都去看電影。
His friend does not go to watch movies every day.

(3)
我們不是每天都有書法課。
We do not have calligraphy class every day.

他的兄弟姐妹不是每個人都喜歡打球。
Not everyone in his siblings likes playing ball.

Questions:
他每個週末都去哪裡運動？
Where does he exercise every weekend?

你爸爸每天都在家吃晚飯嗎？
Does your dad eat dinner at home every day?

他的照片，每張都很好看嗎？
Are all of his photos good-looking?

- Han Han

Usage: 每天 měi tiān is the same as 每一天 měi yī tiān 'every (single) day'. - yī 'one' is often omitted. Similarly, 每個 měi gè is the same as 每一個 měi yī gè 'every (single) one'.

Complete the sentences and dialogues below based on the pictures provided.

A: 我哥哥每天... 每杯... 都喜歡喝咖啡。
My older brother drinks coffee every day... every cup...

B: 他每個週末都去看電影嗎？
Does he go to watch movies every weekend?

A: 他們學校，每棟大樓都很漂亮嗎？
Are all the buildings in their school beautiful?• 当代中文课程

V.
可以 kě yǐ permission
Function: 可以 kě yǐ indicates permission to perform an action.
我妈妈说，你们可以来我家打篮球。
爸爸说，我可以买这支手机。
我叫马安同，你可以叫我小马。

Usage:
1. In Lesson 3 "看电影可以学中文。" Kàn diànyǐng kěyǐ xué Zhōngwén. "Watching movies, (I) can learn Chinese" and in Lesson 5 "你可以教我吗?" Nǐ kěyǐ jiāo wǒ ma? "Can you teach me?", "可以" kěyǐ is used to inquire if it is possible for someone to do something. In this lesson, however, 可以 kěyǐ in the question "我有空可以去看看吗?" Wǒ yǒu kòng kěyǐ qù kànkan ma? "Can I go check it out when I have free time?" is used to ask for permission.
2. 不可以 bù kěyǐ can only be used to indicate "permission", not "possibility". The negative form can only be used to indicate permission. For example, "你不可以说老板不好。" Nǐ bù kěyǐ shuō lǎobǎn bù hǎo. "You mustn't say anything bad about the boss." When you use "可不可以 or 可以不可以", it can indicate either permission or possibility. For example,Lesson: 早上九点去KTV
(Going to KTV at 9 o'clock in the morning)

1. 你可不可以明天来? (possibility)
(Can you come tomorrow?)

2. 我可以不可以买一支新手机? (permission)
(Can I buy a new mobile phone?)

3. When answering a question, "可以" alone suffices.
－
A: 妈妈，我可不可以买这支手机？
B: 可以。
(Mom, can I buy this phone?
Yes, you can.)

请完成以下对话，使用"可以"。
A: 请问咖啡可以外带吗？
B: 外带、内用都可以。
(Excuse me, can I take the coffee to go?
You can take it to go or have it here.)

A: 这杯乌龙茶是谁的？我喝吗？
B: 可以喝！
(Whose cup of oolong tea is this? Can I drink it?
You can drink it!)

A: 请问我们可不可以在这里打篮球？
B: 早上可以，可是晚上不行。
(Excuse me, can we play basketball here?
We can in the morning, but not in the evening.)

A: 这个周末我们去你家看看？
B: 可以，我这个周末没事。
(This weekend, can we come to your house and take a look?
Yes, I'm free this weekend.)

A: 我们在大教室里面吃饭西吗？
B: 不行，但是小教室可以。
(Can we eat sandwiches in the big classroom?
No, but we can in the small classroom.)1. 当代中文课程

III. Daily Activity Survey
Goal: Learning to talk about habitual actions. Learning to tell and ask about time.

Task: Interview your classmates and find out which of the following activities is something they do every day. Write down their names and ask when they usually do these things.

Notes on Pinyin and Pronunciation
Pinyin Rules
When the vowel "u" is followed by "en" and is preceded by a consonant, the "e" is omitted and the spelling becomes "-un". The "e", however, is still pronounced, e.g., jun 君 m Lesson 2.
Chinese Punctuation Marks
Chinese punctuation marks were designed based on the Western system. While a number of them are similar in forms and functions to their Western counterparts, some had been created locally, as shown below.

1. Marks: Pinyin: Functions:
2. 逗號 dòuhào comma
3. 分號 fēnhào semicolon
4. 冒號 màohào colon
5. 驚嘆號 jīngtànhào exclamation mark
6. 問號 wèntíhào question mark
7. 括號 guāhào parentheses
8. 句點 jùdiǎn period/full stop
9. 引號 yǐnhào quotation marks (single)
10. 雙引號 shuāngyǐnhào quotation marks (double)
11. 列舉符号 lièjù fúhào enumeration mark
12. 省略号 shěnglüèhào ellipsis mark
13. 破折号 pòzhéhào em dash
14. 轉名號 zhuǎnmínghào name mark/underscore 
15. 書名號 shūmínghào title marks (book titles)
16. 單標號 dānbiāohào single title marks

II. Asking How with 怎麼 (zěnme)

Function: 怎麼 (zěnme), 'how?', is a question adverb used to ask how something is done.

你們怎麼去？(Nǐmen zěnme qù?) - How do you go?
這個菜怎麼做？(Zhège cài zěnme zuò?) - How is this dish made?
這個歌怎麼唱？(Zhège gē zěnme chàng?) - How is this song sung?
這支新手機怎麼上網？(Zhè zhī xīn shǒujī zěnme shàngwǎng?) - How does this new cellphone connect to the internet?

Usage: 怎麼 (zěnme) 'How?' is quite different from 怎麼樣 (zěnmeyàng) 'How is it? / What do you think?'

怎麼 (zěnme) is an adverb, whereas 怎麼樣 (zěnmeyàng) is a state verb. Compare 這個菜怎麼做？(Zhège cài zěnme zuò?) - "How is this dish made?" with 這個菜怎麼樣？(Zhège cài zěnmeyàng?) - "How is this dish? / What do you think of this dish?"

Describe the manner of action to be carried out in the situations given below.

安同可以怎麼去學校／臺南／故宮／朋友家？(Ān tóng kěyǐ zěnme qù xuéxiào / Táinán / Gùgōng / Péngyǒu jiā?) - How can An Tong go to school / Tainan / National Palace Museum / friend's house?• 當代中文課程
Implicit Comparison with 比較 bǐjiào
Function: The adverb 比較 bǐjiào conveys implicit comparison. The comparison is understood based on the context.
今天比較熱。越南餐廳很遠。坐捷運比較快。
我們家，姐姐比較會做飯。快。
Structures: Adverbs do not take negation directly. Negation goes with the main verb.

Negation:
昨天比較不熱。他比較不喜歡游泳。
我最近比較沒有空。

Questions:
咖啡和茶，你比較喜歡喝咖啡嗎？
你和哥哥，你比較會打棒球嗎？
他比較想去看美國電影還是日本電影？

Usage: In mainland China, 比較 bǐjiào can be used as an adverb meaning 'quite, rather' without a sense of comparison. 他的法文說得比較好。Tā de fǎwén shuō dé bǐjiào hǎo. 'He speaks French rather well.' In Taiwan, 比較 bǐjiào always indicates a comparison. 哥哥和我，我比較高。Gēgē hé wǒ, wǒ bǐjiào gāo. 'Between me and my brother, I am taller.'

Make sentences with 比較 bǐjiào by using the words given in the parentheses. E.g.,
坐計程車比較快。Zuò jìchéngchē bǐjiào kuài. (It would be faster to take a taxi (to get there).)LESSON • 坐火車去臺南

Function: The pattern 又 you 又 you "both A and B" is used to indicate two qualities, situations, or behaviors that are true of the person or thing being discussed.
這家餐廳的菜，既便宜又好吃，所以我們常來吃。
坐高鐵又快又舒服，可是有點貴。
我又想喝茶又想喝咖啡，但是這裡沒有便利店。

V. Comparison 比 bl
Function: The 比 bì preposition indicates an explicit comparison between two items.

山上的風景比這裡漂亮。我們學校比他們學校遠。
坐捷運比坐火車快。

Structures: A 比 B Vs.

Negation: The 比 pattern can be negated by either 不 bù or 不是 bú shì.

在家上網不比在學校快。
我的車不比他的車貴。
坐公車不是比坐計程車快。

Questions:
他們學校比你們學校遠嗎？
這種手機比那種貴嗎？

Usage: In the 比 bǐ pattern, degree adverbs like 很 hěn 'very', 真 zhēn 'really', and 非常 fēicháng 'extraordinary' do not appear before the Vs. It is incorrect, therefore, to say:
我的手機比他的很貴。
坐高鐵比坐火車非常快。A: 週末你想去看電影還是去KTV唱歌？為什麼？
B: 我想去KTV唱歌。我覺得去KTV唱歌比看電影有意思。• 当代中文课程
